<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">North American F-100 Super Sabre</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>

<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:North_American_YF-100_(SN_52-5754)_in_flight_060905-F-1234S-053.jpg"><img alt="Jet aircraft in flight against blue sky; while banking away from camera, it reveals a smooth underside. A long and thin red pitot tube protrudes from the aircraft's nose" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1193" data-file-width="1800" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/North_American_YF-100_%28SN_52-5754%29_in_flight_060905-F-1234S-053.jpg/220px-North_American_YF-100_%28SN_52-5754%29_in_flight_060905-F-1234S-053.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/North_American_YF-100_%28SN_52-5754%29_in_flight_060905-F-1234S-053.jpg/330px-North_American_YF-100_%28SN_52-5754%29_in_flight_060905-F-1234S-053.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/North_American_YF-100_%28SN_52-5754%29_in_flight_060905-F-1234S-053.jpg/440px-North_American_YF-100_%28SN_52-5754%29_in_flight_060905-F-1234S-053.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:North_American_YF-100_(SN_52-5754)_in_flight_060905-F-1234S-053.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The underside of the first of two YF-100A prototypes, s/n <i>52-5754.</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <b>North American F-100 Super Sabre</b> was an American supersonic <a href="/wiki/Jet_engine" title="Jet engine">jet</a> <a href="/wiki/Fighter_aircraft" title="Fighter aircraft">fighter aircraft</a> that served with the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">United States Air Force</a> (USAF) from 1954 to 1971 and with the <a href="/wiki/Air_National_Guard" title="Air National Guard">Air National Guard</a> (ANG) until 1979. The first of the <a href="/wiki/Century_Series" title="Century Series">Century Series</a> of USAF jet fighters, it was the first USAF fighter capable of <a href="/wiki/Supersonic_speed" title="Supersonic speed">supersonic speed</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Level_flight" title="Level flight">level flight</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The F-100 was designed by <a href="/wiki/North_American_Aviation" title="North American Aviation">North American Aviation</a> as a higher performance follow-on to the <a href="/wiki/North_American_F-86_Sabre" title="North American F-86 Sabre">F-86 Sabre</a> air superiority fighter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Adapted as a fighter bomber, the F-100 was supplanted by the Mach two class <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/F-105_Thunderchief" title="F-105 Thunderchief">F-105 Thunderchief</a> for strike missions over North Vietnam. The F-100 flew extensively over South Vietnam as the air force's primary close air support jet until being replaced by the more efficient subsonic <a href="/wiki/LTV_A-7_Corsair_II" title="LTV A-7 Corsair II">LTV A-7 Corsair II</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The F-100 also served in other <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> air forces and with other U.S. allies. In its later life, it was often referred to as the <i>Hun</i>, a shortened version of "one hundred".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Design_and_development">Design and development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_F-100_Super_Sabre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Design and development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:North_American_F-100D_Cockpit_060922-F-1234S-014.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1298" data-file-width="1800" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/North_American_F-100D_Cockpit_060922-F-1234S-014.jpg/220px-North_American_F-100D_Cockpit_060922-F-1234S-014.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/North_American_F-100D_Cockpit_060922-F-1234S-014.jpg/330px-North_American_F-100D_Cockpit_060922-F-1234S-014.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/North_American_F-100D_Cockpit_060922-F-1234S-014.jpg/440px-North_American_F-100D_Cockpit_060922-F-1234S-014.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:North_American_F-100D_Cockpit_060922-F-1234S-014.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The cockpit of an F-100D</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In January 1951, <a href="/wiki/North_American_Aviation" title="North American Aviation">North American Aviation</a> delivered an unsolicited proposal for a supersonic day fighter to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">United States Air Force</a>. Named <i>Sabre 45</i> because of its 45° wing sweep, it represented an evolution of the F-86 Sabre. The <a href="/wiki/Mockup" title="Mockup">mockup</a> was inspected on 7 July 1951, and after over a hundred modifications, the new aircraft was accepted as the F-100 on 30 November 1951. Extensive use of <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a> throughout the aircraft was notable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> On 3 January 1952, the USAF ordered two prototypes followed by 23 F-100As in February and an additional 250 F-100As in August.</p>
<p>The YF-100A first flew on 25 May 1953, seven months ahead of schedule. It reached <a href="/wiki/Mach_number" title="Mach number">Mach 1.05</a> in spite of being fitted with a de-rated <a href="/wiki/Pratt_%26_Whitney_J57" title="Pratt &amp; Whitney J57">XJ57-P-7</a> engine. The second prototype flew on 14 October 1953, followed by the first production F-100A on 9 October 1953. The USAF operational evaluation from November 1953 to December 1955, found the new fighter to have superior performance but declared it not ready for wide-scale deployment due to various deficiencies in the design. These findings were subsequently confirmed during "Project Hot Rod" operational suitability tests.</p>
<p>Particularly troubling was the yaw instability in certain regimes of flight which produced <a href="/wiki/Inertia_coupling" title="Inertia coupling">inertia coupling</a>. The aircraft could develop a sudden yaw and roll which would happen too fast for the pilot to correct and would quickly over-stress the aircraft structure to disintegration. It was under these conditions that North American's chief test pilot, <a href="/wiki/George_Welch_(pilot)" title="George Welch (pilot)">George Welch</a>, was killed while dive testing an early-production F-100A (s/n 52-5764) on 12 October 1954.</p>
<p>Another control problem stemmed from handling characteristics of the <a href="/wiki/Swept_wing" title="Swept wing">swept wing</a> at high <a href="/wiki/Angle_of_attack" title="Angle of attack">angles of attack</a>. As the aircraft approached <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stall_(flight)" title="Stall (flight)">stall</a> speeds, loss of <a href="/wiki/Lift_(force)" title="Lift (force)">lift</a> on the tips of the wings caused a violent <a href="/wiki/Pitch-up" title="Pitch-up">pitch-up</a>. This particular phenomenon (which could easily be fatal at low altitude where there was insufficient time to recover) became known as the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sabre_dance_(aviation)" title="Sabre dance (aviation)">Sabre dance</a>".</p>
<p>Nevertheless, delays in the <a href="/wiki/Republic_F-84F_Thunderstreak" title="Republic F-84F Thunderstreak">Republic F-84F Thunderstreak</a> program pushed the <a href="/wiki/Tactical_Air_Command" title="Tactical Air Command">Tactical Air Command</a> to order the raw F-100A into service. Tactical Air Command also requested that future F-100s be fighter-bombers, with the capability of delivering <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_bomb" title="Nuclear bomb">nuclear bombs</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/North_American_F-107" title="North American F-107">North American F-107</a> was a follow-on Mach 2 development of the F-100 with the air intake moved above and behind the cockpit. It was not produced in quantity in favor of the <a href="/wiki/Republic_F-105_Thunderchief" title="Republic F-105 Thunderchief">Republic F-105 Thunderchief</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Operational_history">Operational history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_F-100_Super_Sabre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Operational history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The F-100A officially entered USAF service on 27 September 1954, with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/479th_Fighter_Wing" title="479th Fighter Wing">479th Fighter Wing</a> at <a href="/wiki/Southern_California_Logistics_Airport" title="Southern California Logistics Airport">George AFB</a>, California. By 10 November 1954, the F-100As suffered six major accidents<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> due to flight instability, structural failures, and hydraulic system failures, prompting the air force to ground the entire fleet until February 1955. The 479th finally became operational in September 1955. Due to ongoing problems, the air force began phasing out the F-100A in 1958, with the last aircraft leaving active duty in 1961. By that time, 47 aircraft had been lost in major accidents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-knaack_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-knaack-2">[2]</a></sup> Escalating tension due to construction of the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Wall" title="Berlin Wall">Berlin Wall</a> in August 1961 forced the USAF to recall the F-100As into active service in early 1962. The aircraft was finally retired in 1970.</p>
<p>The TAC request for a <a href="/wiki/Fighter-bomber" title="Fighter-bomber">fighter-bomber</a> was addressed with the <b>F-100C</b> which flew in March 1954 and entered service on 14 July 1955, with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/450th_Electronic_Warfare_Wing" title="450th Electronic Warfare Wing">450th Fighter Wing</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foster_AFB" title="Foster AFB">Foster AFB</a>, Texas. Operational testing in 1955 revealed that the F-100C was at best an interim solution, sharing all the flaws of the F-100A. The uprated J57-P-21 engine boosted performance but continued to suffer from <a href="/wiki/Compressor_stall" title="Compressor stall">compressor stalls</a>. However, the F-100C was considered an excellent platform for nuclear <a href="/wiki/Toss_bombing" title="Toss bombing">toss bombing</a> because of its high top speed. The inertia coupling problem was reasonably addressed with the installation of a yaw damper in the 146th F-100C, later retrofitted to earlier aircraft. A pitch damper was added starting with the 301st F-100C, at a cost of US$10,000 per aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-knaack_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-knaack-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:North_American_F-100D_060922-F-1234S-002.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1185" data-file-width="1800" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/North_American_F-100D_060922-F-1234S-002.jpg/220px-North_American_F-100D_060922-F-1234S-002.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/North_American_F-100D_060922-F-1234S-002.jpg/330px-North_American_F-100D_060922-F-1234S-002.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/North_American_F-100D_060922-F-1234S-002.jpg/440px-North_American_F-100D_060922-F-1234S-002.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:North_American_F-100D_060922-F-1234S-002.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An F-100D showing its elliptic air intake</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The addition of "wet" hardpoints meant the F-100C could carry a pair of 275 U.S. gal (1,040 l) and a pair of 200 U.S. gal (770 l) drop tanks. However, the combination caused a loss of <a href="/wiki/Directional_stability" title="Directional stability">directional stability</a> at high speeds and the four tanks were soon replaced by a pair of 450 U.S. gal (1,730 l) drop tanks. The 450s proved scarce and expensive and were often replaced by smaller 335 US gal (1,290 l) tanks. Most troubling to TAC was the fact that, as of 1965, only 125 F-100Cs were capable of utilizing all non-nuclear weapons in the air force inventory, particularly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cluster_bomb" title="Cluster bomb">cluster bombs</a> and <a href="/wiki/AIM-9_Sidewinder" title="AIM-9 Sidewinder">AIM-9 Sidewinder</a> air-to-air missiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-knaack_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-knaack-2">[2]</a></sup> By the time the F-100C was phased out in June 1970, 85 had been lost in major accidents.</p>
<p>The definitive <b>F-100D</b> aimed to address the offensive shortcomings of the F-100C by being primarily a ground <a href="/wiki/Attack_aircraft" title="Attack aircraft">attack aircraft</a> with secondary fighter capabilities. To this effect, the aircraft was fitted with <a href="/wiki/Autopilot" title="Autopilot">autopilot</a>, upgraded avionics, and, starting with the 184th production aircraft, <a href="/wiki/AIM-9_Sidewinder" title="AIM-9 Sidewinder">AIM-9 Sidewinder</a> capability. In 1959, 65 aircraft were modified to also fire the <a href="/wiki/AGM-12_Bullpup" title="AGM-12 Bullpup">AGM-12 Bullpup</a> air-to-ground missile. To further address the dangerous flight characteristics, the wing span was extended by 26 in (66 cm) and the vertical tail area was increased by 27%.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:F_100d_56_3238_50tfw_wc_1958.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="434" data-file-width="600" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/F_100d_56_3238_50tfw_wc_1958.jpg/220px-F_100d_56_3238_50tfw_wc_1958.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/F_100d_56_3238_50tfw_wc_1958.jpg/330px-F_100d_56_3238_50tfw_wc_1958.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/F_100d_56_3238_50tfw_wc_1958.jpg/440px-F_100d_56_3238_50tfw_wc_1958.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:F_100d_56_3238_50tfw_wc_1958.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
F-100D of the 417th TFS, 50th TFW post January 1965 ("buzz number" i.e., FW-238 painted over)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first F-100D (54–2121) flew on 24 January 1956, piloted by Daniel Darnell. It entered service on 29 September 1956 with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/405th_Fighter_Wing" title="405th Fighter Wing">405th Fighter Wing</a> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Langley_Air_Force_Base" title="Langley Air Force Base">Langley AFB</a>. The aircraft suffered from reliability problems with the constant speed drive which provides constant-frequency current to the electrical systems. In fact, the drive was so unreliable that the USAF required it to have its own oil system to minimize damage in case of failure. Landing gear and brake parachute malfunctions claimed a number of aircraft, and the refueling probes had a tendency to break away during high speed maneuvers. Numerous post-production fixes created such a diversity of capabilities between individual aircraft that, by 1965, around 700 F-100Ds underwent High Wire modifications to standardize the weapon systems. High Wire modifications took 60 days per aircraft at a cost for the entire project of US $150 million. In 1966, the <i>Combat Skyspot</i> program fitted some F-100Ds with an <a href="/wiki/X_band" title="X band">X band</a> radar transmitter to allow for ground-directed bombing in inclement weather or at night.</p>
<p>In 1961, at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/England_AFB" title="England AFB">England AFB</a>, Louisiana, (<a href="/wiki/401st_Air_Expeditionary_Group" title="401st Air Expeditionary Group">401st Tactical Wing</a>), there were four fighter-bomber squadrons. These were the 612th, 613th, 614th and the 615th (Fighting Tigers). During the Berlin Crisis (approximately September 1961) the 614th was deployed to <a href="/wiki/Ramstein_Air_Base" title="Ramstein Air Base">Ramstein Air Base</a>, Germany, to support the West Germans. At the initial briefing, the 614th personnel were informed that, due to the close proximity of the USSR, if an ICBM were to be launched, they would have only 30 minutes to launch the 614th's aircraft and retire to the nearest German bunker.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:F-100_zero-length-launch_trial.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1189" data-file-width="1800" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/F-100_zero-length-launch_trial.jpg/220px-F-100_zero-length-launch_trial.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/F-100_zero-length-launch_trial.jpg/330px-F-100_zero-length-launch_trial.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/F-100_zero-length-launch_trial.jpg/440px-F-100_zero-length-launch_trial.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:F-100_zero-length-launch_trial.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
F-100D in trial of zero-length-launch system. Pilot is Maj R. Titus. (USAF photo)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1967, the USAF began a structural reinforcement program to extend the aircraft's service life from the designed 3,000 flying hours to 7,000. The USAF alone lost 500 F-100Ds, predominantly in accidents. After one aircraft suffered wing failure, particular attention was paid to lining the wings with external bracing strips. During the Vietnam War, combat losses constituted as many as 50 aircraft per year. On 7 June 1957, an F-100D fitted with an <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Astrodyne&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Astrodyne (page does not exist)">Astrodyne</a> <a href="/wiki/Booster_(rocketry)" title="Booster (rocketry)">booster rocket</a> making 150,000 lbf (667.2 kN) of thrust successfully performed a <a href="/wiki/Zero-length_launch" title="Zero-length launch">zero length launch (ZeLL)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> This was accomplished with the addition of a large canister to the underside of the aircraft. This canister contained a black powder compound and was ignited electro-mechanically, driving the jet engine to minimal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ignition_point" title="Ignition point">ignition point</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The capability was incorporated into late-production aircraft. After a major accident, the USAF Thunderbirds reverted from <a href="/wiki/Republic_F-105_Thunderchief" title="Republic F-105 Thunderchief">F-105 Thunderchiefs</a> to the F-100D which they operated from 1964 until it was replaced by the <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas_F-4_Phantom_II" title="McDonnell Douglas F-4 Phantom II">F-4 Phantom II</a> in 1968.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:F-100c-322fdw.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="413" data-file-width="600" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/F-100c-322fdw.jpg/220px-F-100c-322fdw.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/F-100c-322fdw.jpg/330px-F-100c-322fdw.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/F-100c-322fdw.jpg/440px-F-100c-322fdw.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:F-100c-322fdw.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A USAF KB-50D of the Air Proving Ground Command at Eglin AFB Florida, carrying out the first triple-point refuelling operation with three F-100Cs in 1956</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The F-100 was the subject of many modification programs over the course of its service. Many of these were improvements to electronics, structural strengthening, and projects to improve ease of maintenance. One of these was the replacement of the original afterburners of the J-57 engines with the more advanced afterburners from retired <a href="/wiki/Convair_F-102_Delta_Dagger" title="Convair F-102 Delta Dagger">Convair F-102 Delta Dagger</a> interceptors. This modification changed the appearance of the aft end of the F-100, doing away with the original "petal-style" exhaust. The afterburner modification started in the 1970s and solved maintenance problems with the old type as well as operational problems, including compressor stall issues.</p>
<p>The F-100F two-seat trainer entered service in 1958. It received many of the same weapons and airframe upgrades as the F-100D, including the new afterburners. By 1970, 74 F-100Fs were lost in major accidents.</p>
<p>By 1972, the F-100 was mostly phased out of USAF active service and turned over to tactical fighter groups and squadrons in the ANG. In Air National Guard units, the F-100 was eventually replaced by the F-4 Phantom II, <a href="/wiki/LTV_A-7_Corsair_II" title="LTV A-7 Corsair II">LTV A-7 Corsair II</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fairchild_Republic_A-10_Thunderbolt_II" title="Fairchild Republic A-10 Thunderbolt II">A-10 Thunderbolt II</a>, with the last F-100 retiring in 1979, with the introduction of the <a href="/wiki/General_Dynamics_F-16_Fighting_Falcon" title="General Dynamics F-16 Fighting Falcon">F-16 Fighting Falcon</a>. In foreign service, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Danish_Air_Force" title="Royal Danish Air Force">Royal Danish Air Force</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turkish_Air_Force" title="Turkish Air Force">Turkish Air Force</a> F-100s soldiered on until 1982.</p>
<p>Over the lifetime of its USAF service, a total of 889 F-100 aircraft were destroyed in accidents, involving the deaths of 324 pilots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-afsc_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-afsc-12">[12]</a></sup> The deadliest year for F-100 accidents was 1958, with 116 aircraft destroyed, and 47 pilots killed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-afsc_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-afsc-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>After Super Sabres were withdrawn from service, a large number were converted into remote-controlled drones (QF-100) under the USAF Full Scale Aerial Target (FSAT) program for use as targets for various anti-aircraft weapons, including missile-carrying fighters and fighter-interceptors, with FSAT operations being conducted primarily at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tyndall_AFB" title="Tyndall AFB">Tyndall AFB</a>, Florida. A few F-100s also found their way into civilian hands, primarily with defense contractors supporting USAF and NASA flight test activities at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edwards_AFB" title="Edwards AFB">Edwards AFB</a>, California.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Project_Slick_Chick">Project Slick Chick</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_F-100_Super_Sabre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Project Slick Chick">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:North_American_RF-100A_060905-F-1234S-060.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1125" data-file-width="1800" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/North_American_RF-100A_060905-F-1234S-060.jpg/220px-North_American_RF-100A_060905-F-1234S-060.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/North_American_RF-100A_060905-F-1234S-060.jpg/330px-North_American_RF-100A_060905-F-1234S-060.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/North_American_RF-100A_060905-F-1234S-060.jpg/440px-North_American_RF-100A_060905-F-1234S-060.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:North_American_RF-100A_060905-F-1234S-060.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An RF-100A in flight, inexplicably marked with the AF serial number of a Northrop F-89 Scorpion (53-2600)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:North_American_F-100A-10-NA_Super_Sabre_53-1551.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="319" data-file-width="600" height="117" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/North_American_F-100A-10-NA_Super_Sabre_53-1551.jpg/220px-North_American_F-100A-10-NA_Super_Sabre_53-1551.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/North_American_F-100A-10-NA_Super_Sabre_53-1551.jpg/330px-North_American_F-100A-10-NA_Super_Sabre_53-1551.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/North_American_F-100A-10-NA_Super_Sabre_53-1551.jpg/440px-North_American_F-100A-10-NA_Super_Sabre_53-1551.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:North_American_F-100A-10-NA_Super_Sabre_53-1551.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
North American RF-100A-10-NA Super Sabre 53-1551. Used by Detachment 1 of 7407th Support Sqn of 7499th Support Group. Crashed near Neidenbach, West Germany Oct 1, 1956. Pilot ejected safely.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>North American received a contract to modify six F-100As to RF-100As carrying five cameras, three Fairchild K-17 cameras (see <a href="/wiki/Fairchild_K-20" title="Fairchild K-20">Fairchild K-20</a> camera) in a <a href="/wiki/Trimetrogon" title="Trimetrogon">trimetrogon</a> mounting for photo-mapping and two Fairchild K-38 cameras in a split vertical mounting with the cameras mounted horizontally, shooting via a mirror angled at 45° to reduce the effects of airframe vibrations. All gun armament was removed and the cameras installed in the gun and ammunition bays covered by a bulged fairing under the forward fuselage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slick_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Slick-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>The selected pilots trained on the F-100A at <a href="/wiki/Edwards_Air_Force_Base" title="Edwards Air Force Base">Edwards Air Force Base</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Air_Force_Base" title="George Air Force Base">George Air Force Base</a> in California and then at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palmdale_Air_Force_Base" title="Palmdale Air Force Base">Palmdale Air Force Base</a> for training with the actual RF-100As they would be deployed with. Flight tests revealed that the RF-100A in its intended operational fit of four external tanks was lacking in directional and longitudinal stability, requiring careful handling and close attention to speed limitations for the drop tanks.</p>
<p>Once pilot training was completed in April 1955, three aircraft were deployed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bitburg_Air_Base" title="Bitburg Air Base">Bitburg Air Base</a> in Germany, flying to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brookley_AFB" title="Brookley AFB">Brookley AFB</a> in Mobile, Alabama, cocooned, loaded on an aircraft carrier and delivered to Short Brothers at Sydenham, Belfast, for re-assembly and preparation for flight. At Bitburg they were allocated to Detachment 1 of the 7407th Support Squadron, and commenced operations flying over Eastern Bloc countries at high altitude (over 50,000 ft) to acquire intelligence on military targets. Many attempts were made to intercept these aircraft to no avail, with some photos of fighter airfields clearly showing aircraft climbing for attempted intercepts. The European detachment probably only carried out six missions between mid-1955 and mid-1956 when the <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_U-2" title="Lockheed U-2">Lockheed U-2</a> took over as the deep penetration <a href="/wiki/Aerial_reconnaissance" title="Aerial reconnaissance">aerial reconnaissance</a> asset.</p>
<p>Three RF-100As were also deployed to the <a href="/wiki/6021st_Reconnaissance_Squadron" title="6021st Reconnaissance Squadron">6021st Reconnaissance Squadron</a> at <a href="/wiki/Yokota_Air_Base" title="Yokota Air Base">Yokota Air Base</a> in Japan, but details of operations there are not available. Two RF-100A aircraft were lost in accidents, one due to probable overspeeding which caused the separation of one of the drop tanks and resulted in complete loss of control, and the other due to an engine flame-out. In mid-1958, all four remaining RF-100As were returned to the US and later supplied to the Republic of China Air Force in Taiwan.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Project_High_Wire">Project High Wire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_F-100_Super_Sabre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Project High Wire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:F100DSuperSabreWingsMuseum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/F100DSuperSabreWingsMuseum.jpg/220px-F100DSuperSabreWingsMuseum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/F100DSuperSabreWingsMuseum.jpg/330px-F100DSuperSabreWingsMuseum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/F100DSuperSabreWingsMuseum.jpg/440px-F100DSuperSabreWingsMuseum.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:F100DSuperSabreWingsMuseum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
F-100D-85-NA, AF Ser. No. 56-3417, at <a href="/wiki/Wings_Over_the_Rockies_Air_and_Space_Museum" title="Wings Over the Rockies Air and Space Museum">Wings Over the Rockies Air and Space Museum</a> (former <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lowry_AFB" title="Lowry AFB">Lowry AFB</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Denver,_Colorado" title="Denver, Colorado">Denver, Colorado</a>, painted in its original <a href="/wiki/356th_Tactical_Fighter_Squadron" title="356th Tactical Fighter Squadron">356th TFS</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/354th_TFW" title="354th TFW">354th TFW</a> colors</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Project_High_Wire" title="Project High Wire">Project High Wire</a></div>
<p>"High Wire" was a modernization program for selected F-100Cs, <i>D</i>s and <i>F</i>s. It comprised two modifications: an electrical rewiring upgrade, and a heavy maintenance and IRAN (inspect and repair as necessary) upgrade. Rewiring upgrade operations consisted of replacing old wiring and harnesses with improved maintainable designs. Heavy maintenance and IRAN included new kits, modifications, standardized configurations, repairs, replacements and complete refurbishment.</p>
<p>This project required all new manuals and incremented (i.e. -85 to -86) block numbers. All later production models, especially the <i>F</i> models, included earlier High Wire modifications. New manuals included colored illustrations and had the Roman numeral (I) added after the aircraft number (i.e. T.O. 1F-100D(I)-1S-120, 12 January 1970).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Total production 2,294</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vietnam_War">Vietnam War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_F-100_Super_Sabre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Vietnam War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fighter_and_CAS_missions">Fighter and CAS missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_F-100_Super_Sabre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Fighter and CAS missions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:F-100Ds_416TFS_DaNang_1965.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="274" data-file-width="600" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/F-100Ds_416TFS_DaNang_1965.jpg/220px-F-100Ds_416TFS_DaNang_1965.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/F-100Ds_416TFS_DaNang_1965.jpg/330px-F-100Ds_416TFS_DaNang_1965.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/F-100Ds_416TFS_DaNang_1965.jpg/440px-F-100Ds_416TFS_DaNang_1965.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:F-100Ds_416TFS_DaNang_1965.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
F-100Ds of the 416th Tactical Fighter Squadron at <a href="/wiki/Bien_Hoa_Air_Base" title="Bien Hoa Air Base">Bien Hoa Air Base</a>, South Vietnam, in late June or early July 1965</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:F-100D_firing_rockets_Vietnam_1967.JPEG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="3000" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/F-100D_firing_rockets_Vietnam_1967.JPEG/220px-F-100D_firing_rockets_Vietnam_1967.JPEG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/F-100D_firing_rockets_Vietnam_1967.JPEG/330px-F-100D_firing_rockets_Vietnam_1967.JPEG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/F-100D_firing_rockets_Vietnam_1967.JPEG/440px-F-100D_firing_rockets_Vietnam_1967.JPEG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:F-100D_firing_rockets_Vietnam_1967.JPEG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A USAF F-100D firing rockets in South Vietnam, 1967</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:F-100F_352TFS_35TFW_PhanRang_1971.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="383" data-file-width="600" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/F-100F_352TFS_35TFW_PhanRang_1971.jpg/220px-F-100F_352TFS_35TFW_PhanRang_1971.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/F-100F_352TFS_35TFW_PhanRang_1971.jpg/330px-F-100F_352TFS_35TFW_PhanRang_1971.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/F-100F_352TFS_35TFW_PhanRang_1971.jpg/440px-F-100F_352TFS_35TFW_PhanRang_1971.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:F-100F_352TFS_35TFW_PhanRang_1971.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A USAF F-100F of the 352d TFS at Phu Cat Air Base, South Vietnam, 1971</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 16 April 1961, six Super Sabres were deployed from <a href="/wiki/Clark_Air_Base" title="Clark Air Base">Clark Air Base</a> in the Philippines to Don Muang Airfield in Thailand for air defense purposes; the first F-100s to enter combat in Southeast Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> From that date until their redeployment in 1971, the F-100s were the longest serving U.S. jet <a href="/wiki/Fighter-bomber" title="Fighter-bomber">fighter-bomber</a> to fight in the Vietnam War. Serving as <a href="/wiki/Mikoyan" title="Mikoyan">MiG</a> <a href="/wiki/Combat_air_patrol" title="Combat air patrol">combat air patrol</a> escorts for F-105 Thunderchiefs, Misty <a href="/wiki/Forward_air_control" title="Forward air control">FACs</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wild_weasel" title="Wild weasel">wild weasels</a> over North Vietnam, and then relegated to close air support and ground attacks within South Vietnam.</p>
<p>On 18 August 1964, the first F-100D shot down by ground fire, piloted by 1st Lt Colin A. Clarke, of the <a href="/wiki/428th_Fighter_Squadron" title="428th Fighter Squadron">428th TFS</a>; Clarke ejected and survived. On 4 April 1965, as escorts protecting F-105s attacking the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thanh_Hoa_Bridge" title="Thanh Hoa Bridge">Thanh Hoa Bridge</a>, F-100 Super Sabres fought the USAF's first air-to-air jet combat duel in the Vietnam War, in which an F-100 piloted by Captain Donald W. Kilgus of the <a href="/wiki/416th_Fighter_Squadron" title="416th Fighter Squadron">416th Fighter Squadron</a> shot down a North Vietnamese Air Force <a href="/wiki/Mikoyan-Gurevich_MiG-17" title="Mikoyan-Gurevich MiG-17">MiG-17</a>, using cannon fire, while another fired <a href="/wiki/AIM-9_Sidewinder" title="AIM-9 Sidewinder">Sidewinder</a> missiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The surviving North Vietnamese pilot confirmed three of the MiG-17s had been shot down.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Although recorded by the U.S. Air Force as a probable kill, this represented the first aerial victory by the U.S. Air Force in Vietnam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> However, the small force of four MiG-17s had penetrated the escorting F-100s to claim two F-105s. The F-100 was soon replaced by the F-4C for MiG CAP which pilots noted suffered for lacking built-in guns for dogfights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Vietnam War was not known for utilizing activated <a href="/wiki/National_Guard_of_the_United_States" title="National Guard of the United States">Army National Guard, Air National Guard</a> or other U.S. Reserve units; but rather, had a reputation for <a href="/wiki/Conscription" title="Conscription">conscription</a> during the course of the war. During a confirmation hearing before <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">Congress</a> in 1973, USAF general, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_S._Brown" title="George S. Brown">George S. Brown</a>, who had commanded the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/7th_Air_Force" title="7th Air Force">7th Air Force</a> during the war, stated that five of the best Super Sabre squadrons in Vietnam were from the ANG.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> This included the 120th Tactical Fighter Squadron (120 TFS) of the <a href="/wiki/Colorado_Air_National_Guard" title="Colorado Air National Guard">Colorado Air National Guard</a>, the 136 TFS of the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Air_National_Guard" title="New York Air National Guard">New York Air National Guard</a> TFS, the 174 TFS of the <a href="/wiki/Iowa_Air_National_Guard" title="Iowa Air National Guard">Iowa Air National Guard</a> and the 188 TFS of the <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico_Air_National_Guard" title="New Mexico Air National Guard">New Mexico Air National Guard</a>. The fifth unit was a regular AF squadron manned by mostly air national guardsmen.</p>
<p>The Air National Guard F-100 squadrons increased the regular USAF by nearly 100 Super Sabres in theater, averaging, for the Colorado ANG F-100s, 24 missions a day, delivering ordnance and munitions with a 99.5% reliability rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> From May 1968 to April 1969, the ANG Super Sabres flew more than 38,000 combat hours and more than 24,000 sorties. Between them, at the cost of seven F-100 Air Guard pilots killed (plus one staff officer) and the loss of 14 Super Sabres to enemy action, the squadrons expended over four million rounds of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/20mm" title="20mm">20mm</a> shells, 30 million pounds of bombs and over 10 million pounds of <a href="/wiki/Napalm" title="Napalm">napalm</a> against their enemy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Hun was also deployed as a two-seat F-100F model which saw service as a "fast FAC" or <i>Misty</i> FAC (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forward_air_controller" title="Forward air controller">forward air controller</a>) in North Vietnam and Laos, spotting targets for other fighter-bomber aircraft, performing road reconnaissance, and conducting SAR (search and rescue) missions as part of the top-secret project, Commando Sabre, based out of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phu_Cat_Air_Base" title="Phu Cat Air Base">Phu Cat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tuy_Hoa_Air_Base" title="Tuy Hoa Air Base">Tuy Hoa</a> air bases.</p>
<p>By the war's end, 242 F-100 Super Sabres had been lost in Vietnam, as the F-100 was progressively replaced by the F-4 Phantom II and the F-105 Thunderchief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> The Hun had logged 360,283 combat sorties during the war and its wartime operations came to end on 31 July 1971.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> The four fighter wings with F-100s flew more combat sorties in Vietnam than over 15,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P-51_Mustang" title="P-51 Mustang">P-51 Mustangs</a> flew during World War II. After 1965, they did not fly into North Vietnam and mainly performed close air support missions. Despite the April 1965 dogfight, the air force classified the engagement as resulting in a "probable" kill, and no F-100 was ever officially credited with any aerial victories. No F-100 in Vietnam was lost to enemy fighters, but 186 were shot down by anti-aircraft fire, seven were destroyed from Vietcong attacks on airbases, and 45 crashed in operational incidents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Wild_Weasel">Wild Weasel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_F-100_Super_Sabre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Wild Weasel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>It was also the first <a href="/wiki/Wild_Weasel" title="Wild Weasel">Wild Weasel</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SEAD" title="SEAD">SEAD</a> (air defense suppression) aircraft whose specially trained crews were tasked with locating and destroying enemy air defenses. Four F-100F Wild Weasel Is were fitted with an APR-25 vector radar homing and warning (RHAW) receiver, IR-133 panoramic receivers with greater detection range, and KA-60 panoramic cameras. The APR-25 could detect early-warning radars and emissions from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SA-2_Guideline" title="SA-2 Guideline">SA-2 Guideline</a> tracking and guidance systems. These aircraft deployed to <a href="/wiki/Korat_Royal_Thai_Air_Force_Base" title="Korat Royal Thai Air Force Base">Korat Royal Thai Air Force Base</a>, Thailand, in November 1965, began flying combat missions with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/388th_Tactical_Fighter_Wing" title="388th Tactical Fighter Wing">388th Tactical Fighter Wing</a> in December. They were joined by three more aircraft in February 1966. All Wild Weasel F-100Fs were eventually modified to fire the <a href="/wiki/AGM-45_Shrike" title="AGM-45 Shrike">AGM-45 Shrike</a> anti-radiation missile.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Algerian_war">Algerian war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_F-100_Super_Sabre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Algerian war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tr-f100.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="570" data-file-width="870" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Tr-f100.jpg/220px-Tr-f100.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Tr-f100.jpg/330px-Tr-f100.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Tr-f100.jpg/440px-Tr-f100.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tr-f100.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
F-100D 54-2122 (the second receipt by the French Air Force) with the colors of the 3/11 "Corsica" at Toul-Rosières Air Base in June 1970.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>French Air Force Super Sabres might have flown combat missions, with strikes flown from bases within France against targets in Algeria. The planes were based at <a href="/wiki/Reims_%E2%80%93_Champagne_Air_Base" title="Reims – Champagne Air Base">Reims</a>, refuelling at <a href="/wiki/Istres-Le_Tub%C3%A9_Air_Base" title="Istres-Le Tubé Air Base">Istres</a> on the return flight from attacking targets in Algeria. The F-100 was the main fighter-bomber in the French Air Force during the 1960s, until it was replaced by the <a href="/wiki/SEPECAT_Jaguar" title="SEPECAT Jaguar">Jaguar</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cyprus_crisis">Cyprus crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_F-100_Super_Sabre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Cyprus crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Turkish Air Force F-100 units were used during the <a href="/wiki/Turkish_invasion_of_Cyprus" title="Turkish invasion of Cyprus">Turkish invasion of Cyprus</a> in 1974. Together with <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_F-104_Starfighter" title="Lockheed F-104 Starfighter">F-104G Starfighters</a>, they provided close air support to Turkish ground troops and bombed targets around Nicosia.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taiwan">Taiwan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_F-100_Super_Sabre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Taiwan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Taiwanese Super Sabres flew reconnaissance missions over the Chinese mainland. Several F-100s were reported shot down or crashed on these missions. These sorties were done in secrecy and it is supposed that the CIA or USAF supported or knew of them.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Achievements">Achievements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_F-100_Super_Sabre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Achievements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:North_American_F-100D_Super_Sabre_USAF.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1068" data-file-width="1800" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/North_American_F-100D_Super_Sabre_USAF.jpg/220px-North_American_F-100D_Super_Sabre_USAF.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/North_American_F-100D_Super_Sabre_USAF.jpg/330px-North_American_F-100D_Super_Sabre_USAF.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/North_American_F-100D_Super_Sabre_USAF.jpg/440px-North_American_F-100D_Super_Sabre_USAF.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:North_American_F-100D_Super_Sabre_USAF.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An F-100D of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force_Thunderbirds" title="United States Air Force Thunderbirds">United States Air Force Thunderbirds</a> on display at the <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_the_United_States_Air_Force" title="National Museum of the United States Air Force">National Museum of the United States Air Force</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Source: Knaack<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-knaack_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-knaack-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>The first operational aircraft in <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">United States Air Force</a> inventory capable of exceeding the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_sound" title="Speed of sound">speed of sound</a> in level flight.</li>
<li>On 29 October 1953, the first YF-100A prototype set a world speed record of 755.149 mph (656.207 kn, 1,215.295 km/h) at low altitude.</li>
<li>On 20 August 1955, an F-100C set the first supersonic world speed record of 822.135 mph (714.416 kn, 1,323.098 km/h).</li>
<li>On 4 September 1955, an F-100C won the <a href="/wiki/Bendix_Trophy" title="Bendix Trophy">Bendix Trophy</a>, covering 2,235 mi (2,020 nmi, 3,745 km) at an average speed of 610.726 mph (530.706 kn, 982.868 km/h).</li>
<li>On 26 December 1956, two F-100Ds became the first-ever aircraft to successfully perform buddy refueling.</li>
<li>On 13 May 1957, three F-100Cs set a new world distance record for single-engine aircraft by covering the 6,710 mi (5,835 nmi, 10,805 km) distance from <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> to <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a> in 14 hours and 4 minutes. The flight was accomplished using inflight refueling.</li>
<li>On 7 August 1959, two F-100Fs became the first-ever jet fighters to fly over the <a href="/wiki/North_Pole" title="North Pole">North Pole</a>.</li>
<li>On 16 April 1961, the first USAF combat jets to enter the Vietnam War.</li>
<li>On 4 April 1965, the first USAF aircraft to engage in aerial jet combat during the Vietnam War, while escorting F-105 Thunderchiefs to target.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force_Thunderbirds" title="United States Air Force Thunderbirds">United States Air Force Thunderbirds</a> operated the F-100C from 1956 until 1964. After briefly converting to the F-105 Thunderchief, the team flew F-100Ds from July 1964 until November 1968, before converting to the F-4E Phantom II.</li>
</ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Variants">Variants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_F-100_Super_Sabre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Variants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:QF-100D_drone_near_Tyndall_AFB_1986.JPEG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1860" data-file-width="2870" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/QF-100D_drone_near_Tyndall_AFB_1986.JPEG/220px-QF-100D_drone_near_Tyndall_AFB_1986.JPEG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/QF-100D_drone_near_Tyndall_AFB_1986.JPEG/330px-QF-100D_drone_near_Tyndall_AFB_1986.JPEG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/QF-100D_drone_near_Tyndall_AFB_1986.JPEG/440px-QF-100D_drone_near_Tyndall_AFB_1986.JPEG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:QF-100D_drone_near_Tyndall_AFB_1986.JPEG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A QF-100D pilotless drone near Tyndall Air Force Base, Florida, in 1986.</div>
</div>
</div>
<dl>
<dt>YF-100A</dt>
<dd>Prototype, model NA-180 two built, s/n 52-5754 and 5755.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson-27">[27]</a></sup></dd>
<dt>YQF-100</dt>
<dd>Nine test unmanned drone version: two D-models, one YQF-100F F-model,<sup>see DF-100F</sup>, and six other test versions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baugher100_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baugher100-28">[28]</a></sup></dd>
<dt>F-100A</dt>
<dd>Single-seat day fighter; 203 built, model NA-192.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson-27">[27]</a></sup></dd>
<dt>RF-100A ("Slick Chick")</dt>
<dd>Six F-100A aircraft modified for photo reconnaissance in 1954. Unarmed, with camera installations in lower fuselage bay. Used for overflights of Soviet Bloc countries in Europe and the Far-East. Retired from USAF service in 1958, the surviving four aircraft were transferred to the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China_Air_Force" title="Republic of China Air Force">Republic of China Air Force</a> and retired in 1960.</dd>
<dt>F-100B</dt>
<dd>See <a href="/wiki/North_American_F-107" title="North American F-107">North American F-107</a></dd>
<dt>F-100BI</dt>
<dd>Proposed interceptor version of F-100B, did not advance beyond mock-up.</dd>
<dt>F-100C</dt>
<dd>Seventy Model NA-214 and 381 Model NA-217.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson-27">[27]</a></sup> Additional fuel tanks in the wings, fighter-bomber capability, <a href="/wiki/Aerial_refueling" title="Aerial refueling">probe-and-drogue</a> refueling capability, uprated J57-P-21 engine on late production aircraft. First flight: March 1954; 476 built.</dd>
<dt>TF-100C</dt>
<dd>One F-100C converted into a two-seat training aircraft.</dd>
<dt>F-100D</dt>
<dd>Single-seat fighter-bomber, more advanced avionics, larger wing and tail fin, landing flaps. First flight: 24 January 1956; 1,274 built.</dd>
<dt>F-100F</dt>
<dd>Two-seat training version, armament decreased from four to two cannon. First flight: 7 March 1957; 339 built.</dd>
<dt>DF-100F</dt>
<dd>This designation was given to one F-100F that was used as drone director.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baugher100_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Baugher100-28">[28]</a></sup></dd>
<dt>NF-100F</dt>
<dd>Three F-100Fs used for test purposes, the prefix "N" indicates that modifications prevented return to regular operational service.</dd>
<dt>TF-100F</dt>
<dd>Specific Danish designation given to 14 F-100Fs exported to Denmark in 1974, in order to distinguish these from the 10 F-100Fs delivered 1959–1961.</dd>
<dt>QF-100</dt>
<dd>Another 209 D and F models were ordered and converted to unmanned radio-controlled FSAT (full scale aerial target)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> drone and drone directors for testing and destruction by modern air-to-air missiles used by current U.S. Air Force fighter jets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baugher100_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-Baugher100-28">[28]</a></sup></dd>
<dt>F-100J</dt>
<dd>Unbuilt all-weather export version for Japan</dd>
<dt>F-100K</dt>
<dd>Unbuilt design study for a two-seat F-100F powered by a J57-P-55 engine</dd>
<dt>F-100L</dt>
<dd>Unbuilt design study for a single-seat F-100D powered by a J57-P-55 engine</dd>
<dt>F-100N</dt>
<dd>Unbuilt version with simplified avionics for NATO customers</dd>
<dt>F-100S</dt>
<dd>Proposed French-built F-100F with <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Spey" title="Rolls-Royce Spey">Rolls-Royce Spey</a> <a href="/wiki/Turbofan" title="Turbofan">turbofan</a> engine</dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Operators">Operators</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_F-100_Super_Sabre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Operators">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl>
<dt><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Flag_of_the_Republic_of_China.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_Republic_of_China.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Flag_of_the_Republic_of_China.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_Republic_of_China.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Flag_of_the_Republic_of_China.svg/45px-Flag_of_the_Republic_of_China.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Republic of China (Taiwan)</a></dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China_Air_Force" title="Republic of China Air Force">Republic of China Air Force</a></li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>It was the only allied air force to operate the F-100A model. The first F-100 was delivered in October 1958. It was followed by 15 F-100As in 1959, and by 65 more F-100As in 1960. In 1961, four unarmed RF-100As were delivered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Additionally, 38 ex-USAF/Air National Guard F-100As were delivered later, to bring the total strength to 118 F-100As and four RF-100As. F-100As were retrofitted with the F-100D vertical tail with its AN/APS-54 tail-warning radar and equipped to launch Sidewinder air-to-air missiles. Several were lost in intelligence missions over the People's Republic of China.</dd>
</dl>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:N.A._F-100D_G-261_42261_RDAF_WADD_09.65_edited-2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="725" data-file-width="1024" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/N.A._F-100D_G-261_42261_RDAF_WADD_09.65_edited-2.jpg/220px-N.A._F-100D_G-261_42261_RDAF_WADD_09.65_edited-2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/N.A._F-100D_G-261_42261_RDAF_WADD_09.65_edited-2.jpg/330px-N.A._F-100D_G-261_42261_RDAF_WADD_09.65_edited-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/N.A._F-100D_G-261_42261_RDAF_WADD_09.65_edited-2.jpg/440px-N.A._F-100D_G-261_42261_RDAF_WADD_09.65_edited-2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:N.A._F-100D_G-261_42261_RDAF_WADD_09.65_edited-2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Operational Danish AF F-100D Super Sabre in 1965</div>
</div>
</div>
<dl>
<dt><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="280" data-file-width="370" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Flag_of_Denmark.svg/20px-Flag_of_Denmark.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Flag_of_Denmark.svg/31px-Flag_of_Denmark.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Flag_of_Denmark.svg/40px-Flag_of_Denmark.svg.png 2x" width="20"/> </span>Denmark</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Royal_Danish_Air_Force" title="Royal Danish Air Force">Royal Danish Air Force</a></li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd><i>Flyvevåbnet</i> operated a total of 72 aircraft. 48 F-100Ds and 10 Fs were delivered to Denmark from 1959 to 1961 as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mutual_Defense_Assistance_Program" title="Mutual Defense Assistance Program">MDAP</a> equipment. The F-100 replaced the <a href="/wiki/Republic_F-84_Thunderjet" title="Republic F-84 Thunderjet">F-84G Thunderjet</a> as a strike fighter in three squadrons; 725, 727 and 730. The F-100s of <i><a href="/wiki/Squadron_(aviation)" title="Squadron (aviation)">Eskadrille</a></i> 725 were replaced by <a href="/wiki/Saab_35_Draken" title="Saab 35 Draken">Saab F-35 Draken</a> in 1970 and in 1974 14 two-seated ex-USAF TF-100F were bought. The last Danish F-100s were retired from service in 1982, replaced by <a href="/wiki/General_Dynamics_F-16_Fighting_Falcon" title="General Dynamics F-16 Fighting Falcon">F-16s</a>. The surviving MDAP F-100s were transferred to Turkey (21 F-100Ds and two F-100Fs), while six TF-100Fs were sold for target towing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schr.C3.B8der_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schr.C3.B8der-32">[32]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c3/Flag_of_France.svg/23px-Flag_of_France.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c3/Flag_of_France.svg/35px-Flag_of_France.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c3/Flag_of_France.svg/45px-Flag_of_France.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span>France</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/French_Air_Force" title="French Air Force">French Air Force</a></li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>The <i>Armee de l'Air</i> was the first Western-aligned air force to receive the F-100 Super Sabre. The first aircraft arrived in France on 1 May 1958. A total of 100 aircraft (85 F-100Ds and 15 F-100Fs) were supplied to France, and assigned to the NATO 4th Allied Tactical Air Force. They were stationed at German-French bases. French F-100s were used on combat missions flying from bases in France against targets in Algeria. In 1967, France left NATO, and German-based F-100s were transferred to France, using bases vacated by the USAF. The last unit on F100D/F was the Escadron 4/11 Jura, based at Djibouti, which kept the Super Sabre until 1978.</dd>
</dl>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:North_American_F-100_Super_Sabre,_TuAF.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/North_American_F-100_Super_Sabre%2C_TuAF.jpg/220px-North_American_F-100_Super_Sabre%2C_TuAF.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/North_American_F-100_Super_Sabre%2C_TuAF.jpg/330px-North_American_F-100_Super_Sabre%2C_TuAF.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/North_American_F-100_Super_Sabre%2C_TuAF.jpg/440px-North_American_F-100_Super_Sabre%2C_TuAF.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:North_American_F-100_Super_Sabre,_TuAF.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
North American F-100 Super Sabre of the Turkish Air Force displayed at Istanbul Aviation Museum, Turkey</div>
</div>
</div>
<dl>
<dt><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1200" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Flag_of_Turkey.svg/23px-Flag_of_Turkey.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Flag_of_Turkey.svg/35px-Flag_of_Turkey.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Flag_of_Turkey.svg/45px-Flag_of_Turkey.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span>Turkey</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Turkish_Air_Force" title="Turkish Air Force">Turkish Air Force</a></li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>The <i>Turk Hava Kuvvetleri</i> received 206 F-100C, D and F Super Sabres. Most came from USAF stocks, and 21 F-100Ds and two F-100Fs were supplied by Denmark. Turkish F-100s saw extensive action during the 1974 military operation against Cyprus.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="650" data-file-width="1235" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/46px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span>United States</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">United States Air Force</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force_Thunderbirds" title="United States Air Force Thunderbirds">United States Air Force Thunderbirds</a></li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/List_of_F-100_units_of_the_United_States_Air_Force" title="List of F-100 units of the United States Air Force">List of F-100 units of the United States Air Force</a></dd>
</dl>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Surviving_aircraft">Surviving aircraft</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_F-100_Super_Sabre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Surviving aircraft">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Denmark">Denmark</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_F-100_Super_Sabre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Denmark">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:F-100-DK-01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="751" data-file-width="1024" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/F-100-DK-01.jpg/220px-F-100-DK-01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/F-100-DK-01.jpg/330px-F-100-DK-01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/F-100-DK-01.jpg/440px-F-100-DK-01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:F-100-DK-01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Retired RDAF F-100F Super Sabre</div>
</div>
</div>
<dl>
<dt>F-100F</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>56-3927/GT-927 - Denmark Flying Museum, <a href="/wiki/Billund,_Denmark" title="Billund, Denmark">Billund</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="France">France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_F-100_Super_Sabre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<dl>
<dt>F-100D</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>55-2736 - <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_de_l%27Air_et_de_l%27Espace" title="Musée de l'Air et de l'Espace">Musée de l'Air et de l'Espace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a> / <a href="/wiki/Le_Bourget" title="Le Bourget">Le Bourget</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Germany">Germany</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_F-100_Super_Sabre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Germany">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<dl>
<dt>F-100D</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>54-2136 <a href="/wiki/French_Air_Force" title="French Air Force">French Air Force</a> - Schwäbisches Bauern Technical Museum, <a href="/wiki/Eschach,_Germany" title="Eschach, Germany">Eschach</a>-Seifertshofen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></li>
<li>54-2185 <a href="/wiki/French_Air_Force" title="French Air Force">French Air Force</a> - Schwäbisches Bauern Technical Museum, <a href="/wiki/Eschach,_Germany" title="Eschach, Germany">Eschach</a>-Seifertshofen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dt>F-100F</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>56-3944 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">United States Air Force</a> - <a href="/wiki/Flugausstellung_Hermeskeil" title="Flugausstellung Hermeskeil">Flugausstellung Leo Junior</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hermeskeil" title="Hermeskeil">Hermeskeil</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Netherlands">Netherlands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_F-100_Super_Sabre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Netherlands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<dl>
<dt>F-100D</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>54-2265 <a href="/wiki/French_Air_Force" title="French Air Force">French Air Force</a> (painted as 54-1871) - <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Militaire_Luchtvaartmuseum" title="Militaire Luchtvaartmuseum">Militaire Luchtvaartmuseum</a></i>, Kamp van <a href="/wiki/Zeist" title="Zeist">Zeist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soesterberg" title="Soesterberg">Soesterberg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>. It was returned to USAF, repainted in USAF markings and in 1976 to gate guardian at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RAF_Wethersfield" title="RAF Wethersfield">RAF Wethersfield</a>, <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>. It was then removed 20 January 1988 and reported at the time to be destined for AMARC, to be held in storage on behalf of USAFM (now NMUSAF).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></li>
</ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Specifications_.28F-100D.29">Specifications (F-100D)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_American_F-100_Super_Sabre&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Specifications (F-100D)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="floatright"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:F-100_Super_Sabre_3-view.svg" title="Orthographically projected diagram of the F-100 Super Sabre."><img alt="Orthographically projected diagram of the F-100 Super Sabre." data-file-height="493" data-file-width="706" height="279" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/F-100_Super_Sabre_3-view.svg/400px-F-100_Super_Sabre_3-view.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/F-100_Super_Sabre_3-view.svg/600px-F-100_Super_Sabre_3-view.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/F-100_Super_Sabre_3-view.svg/800px-F-100_Super_Sabre_3-view.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:F100D_563417nose2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/F100D_563417nose2.jpg/220px-F100D_563417nose2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/F100D_563417nose2.jpg/330px-F100D_563417nose2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/F100D_563417nose2.jpg/440px-F100D_563417nose2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:F100D_563417nose2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
D-model nose shot, oval intake</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:North_American_F-100D_Cockpit_060922-F-1234S-014.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1298" data-file-width="1800" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/North_American_F-100D_Cockpit_060922-F-1234S-014.jpg/220px-North_American_F-100D_Cockpit_060922-F-1234S-014.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/North_American_F-100D_Cockpit_060922-F-1234S-014.jpg/330px-North_American_F-100D_Cockpit_060922-F-1234S-014.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/North_American_F-100D_Cockpit_060922-F-1234S-014.jpg/440px-North_American_F-100D_Cockpit_060922-F-1234S-014.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:North_American_F-100D_Cockpit_060922-F-1234S-014.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>

<p style="font-size: 90%; margin:0; padding: .2em"><i>Data from</i> <i>Quest for Performance</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<p><span style="font-size: 120%;"><b>General characteristics</b></span></p>
<ul>
<li><b>Crew:</b> 1</li>
<li><b>Length:</b> 50 ft (15.2 m)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Wingspan" title="Wingspan">Wingspan</a>:</b> 38 ft 9 in (11.81 m)</li>
<li><b>Height:</b> 16 ft 2¾ in (4.95 m)</li>
<li><b>Wing area:</b> 400 ft² (37 m²)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Manufacturer%27s_empty_weight" title="Manufacturer's empty weight">Empty weight</a>:</b> 21,000 <a href="/wiki/Pound_(mass)" title="Pound (mass)">lb</a> (9,500 kg)</li>
<li><b>Loaded weight:</b> 28,847 lb (13,085 kg)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Maximum_takeoff_weight" title="Maximum takeoff weight">Max. takeoff weight</a>:</b> 34,832 lb (15,800 kg)</li>
<li>*<b><a href="/wiki/Zero-lift_drag_coefficient" title="Zero-lift drag coefficient">Zero-lift drag coefficient</a>:</b> 0.0130</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Zero-lift_drag_coefficient" title="Zero-lift drag coefficient">Drag area:</a></b> 5.0 ft² (0.46 m²)</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aspect_ratio_(wing)" title="Aspect ratio (wing)">Aspect ratio</a>:</b> 3.76</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Aircraft_engine" title="Aircraft engine">Powerplant</a>:</b> 1 × <a href="/wiki/Pratt_%26_Whitney_J57" title="Pratt &amp; Whitney J57">Pratt &amp; Whitney J57</a>-P-21/21A <a href="/wiki/Turbojet" title="Turbojet">turbojet</a>
<ul>
<li><b>Dry thrust:</b> 10,200 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pound-force" title="Pound-force">lbf</a> (45 kN)</li>
<li><b>Thrust with <a href="/wiki/Afterburner" title="Afterburner">afterburner</a>:</b> 16,000 lbf (71 kN)</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<p><span style="font-size: 120%;"><b>Performance</b></span></p>
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/V_speeds#Regulatory_V-speeds" title="V speeds">Maximum speed</a>:</b> 750 <a href="/wiki/Knot_(unit)" title="Knot (unit)">kn</a> (864 mph, 1,390 km/h, Mach 1.3)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Range_(aeronautics)" title="Range (aeronautics)">Range</a>:</b> 1,733 <a href="/wiki/Nautical_mile" title="Nautical mile">NM</a> (1,995 mi, 3,210 km)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Ceiling_(aeronautics)" title="Ceiling (aeronautics)">Service ceiling</a>:</b> 50,000 ft (15,000 m)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Rate_of_climb" title="Rate of climb">Rate of climb</a>:</b> 22,400 ft/min (114 m/s)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Wing_loading" title="Wing loading">Wing loading</a>:</b> 72.1 lb/ft² (352 kg/m²)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Thrust-to-weight_ratio" title="Thrust-to-weight ratio">Thrust/weight</a>:</b> 0.55<br/></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Lift-to-drag_ratio" title="Lift-to-drag ratio">Lift-to-drag ratio</a>:</b> 13.9</li>
</ul>
<p><span style="font-size: 120%;"><b>Armament</b></span><br/></p>
<ul>
<li><b>Guns:</b> 4× <a href="/wiki/20_mm_caliber" title="20 mm caliber">20 mm (0.787 in)</a> <a href="/wiki/M39_cannon" title="M39 cannon">Pontiac M39A1</a> <a href="/wiki/Revolver_cannon" title="Revolver cannon">revolver cannon</a></li>
<li><b>Missiles:</b> ** 4× <a href="/wiki/AIM-9_Sidewinder" title="AIM-9 Sidewinder">AIM-9 Sidewinder</a> <i>or</i>
<ul>
<li>2× <a href="/wiki/AGM-12_Bullpup" title="AGM-12 Bullpup">AGM-12 Bullpup</a> <i>or</i></li>
<li>2× or 4× <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mk_4/Mk_40_Folding-Fin_Aerial_Rocket#US_Mk_40_FFAR_Launchers" title="Mk 4/Mk 40 Folding-Fin Aerial Rocket">LAU-3/A</a> 2.75" unguided rocket dispenser<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><b>Bombs:</b> 7,040 lb (3,190 kg) of weapons, including
<ul>
<li>Conventional bombs <i>or</i></li>
<li>Special stores:
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mark_7_nuclear_bomb" title="Mark 7 nuclear bomb">Mark 7 nuclear bomb</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASM_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASM-153">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup><i>or</i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/B28_nuclear_bomb" title="B28 nuclear bomb">Mk 28 nuclear bomb</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASM_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASM-153">[153]</a></sup> <i>or</i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/B28_nuclear_bomb" title="B28 nuclear bomb">Mk 38 nuclear bomb</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASM_153-2"><a href="#cite_note-NASM-153">[153]</a></sup> <i>or</i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/B43_nuclear_bomb" title="B43 nuclear bomb">Mk 43 nuclear bomb</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASM_153-3"><a href="#cite_note-NASM-153">[153]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<p><span style="font-size: 120%;"><b>Avionics</b></span><br/></p>
<ul>
<li>Minneapolis-Honeywell MB-3 automatic pilot<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASM_153-4"><a href="#cite_note-NASM-153">[153]</a></sup></li>
<li>AN/AJB-1B low-altitude bombing system<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASM_153-5"><a href="#cite_note-NASM-153">[153]</a></sup></li>
<li>AN/APR-26 rearward radar warning<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASM_153-6"><a href="#cite_note-NASM-153">[153]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
